<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15697602829634258855">Set in contemporary San Francisco and in a Chinese village where Peking Man is unearthed, "The Bonesetter's Daughter" is an excavation of the human spirit: the past, its deepest wounds, its most profound hopes. This is the story of LuLing Young, who searches for the name of her mother, the daughter of the famous Bonesetter from the Mouth of the Mountain. The story conjures</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15697602829634258855">Set in contemporary San Francisco and in a Chinese village where Peking Man is unearthed, "The Bonesetter's Daughter" is an excavation of the human spirit: the past, its deepest wounds, its most profound hopes. This is the story of LuLing Young, who searches for the name of her mother, the daughter of the famous Bonesetter from the Mouth of the Mountain. The story conjures the pain of broken dreams, the power of myths, and the strength of love that enables us to recover in memory what we have lost in grief.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15697602829634258855">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_9369829">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9369829" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/135316-amelia" class="left imgcol" title="Amelia"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1299461166p2/135316.jpg" alt="Amelia" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/9369829?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 24, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/135316-amelia" class="user" title="Amelia">Amelia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Mom, Kristen, Celeste</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Amelia by: </span>
      Diane and Susan
  </div>



    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer9369829">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15585114262226321012">Amy Tan has a way of starting a story that's impossible to put down. For the first half of the book I kept wondering what about it made it so good. Anecdotal stories, relatable characters, Chinese folklore for interest ... these are all good, but I finally realized in the last quarter of the book why I liked it so much. Because it's a book about learning to love your past no matter how many scars it gives you, and learning to love and forgive your parents and ancestors, no matter what they may h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15585114262226321012">Amy Tan has a way of starting a story that's impossible to put down. For the first half of the book I kept wondering what about it made it so good. Anecdotal stories, relatable characters, Chinese folklore for interest ... these are all good, but I finally realized in the last quarter of the book why I liked it so much. Because it's a book about learning to love your past no matter how many scars it gives you, and learning to love and forgive your parents and ancestors, no matter what they may have done to your gene pool. It's a story about loving people the best way you know how, and believing that some day they'll know just how much you love them, and just how much you wish you could change your faults so you could love them better. But you hope that your feeble offering will be enough. And it's a story about accepting the feeble offering for the gold mine that it is ... not feeble at all. I learned a lot about myself and my family relationships through reading this book, and would recommend it to anyone who has a loved one they just can't quite relate to or understand. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15585114262226321012">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_33122117">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33122117" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/185835-yulia" class="left imgcol" title="Yulia"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1375640372p2/185835.jpg" alt="Yulia" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/33122117?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 07, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/185835-yulia" class="user" title="Yulia">Yulia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/185835-yulia?shelf=writers-on-writing" class="actionLinkLite">writers-on-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer33122117">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9222294057579649007">This is a chronicle of voicelessness across three generations of a Chinese family: it captures how these women lost their voices, why they continued to be voiceless, and how they attempted to reclaim their voice. Voice in this book is both literal and figurative: it's about standing up for oneself, speaking one's truth, being acknowledged, being understood, and not being censored. And the perpetrators who claim the women's voices can be cultural, personal (through the violation of one's secrets</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9222294057579649007">This is a chronicle of voicelessness across three generations of a Chinese family: it captures how these women lost their voices, why they continued to be voiceless, and how they attempted to reclaim their voice. Voice in this book is both literal and figurative: it's about standing up for oneself, speaking one's truth, being acknowledged, being understood, and not being censored. And the perpetrators who claim the women's voices can be cultural, personal (through the violation of one's secrets or body), cross-cultural, as what happens to the youngest when she finds herself in a relationship with a man who already has two Caucasian children, and even professional, as what happens for those who choose to give voice to others' ideas but not their own (as ghostwriters). And not incidentally, it is also a book about ghosts who remain with us from our past, haunting us with their curses or benevolently giving us advice about our current choices. Serendipitously enough, this book made me proud to be part-Chinese, but also sad that there was so much about Chinese culture and especially its writing and its calligraphy that I cannot understand. But in the end, it inspires the reader to speak out, to express appreciation to relatives, to insist on being heard in one's relationship, and to rediscover the paths of their ancestors. It may sound corny, but this book was an incredibly moving read for me, unsettling me and making me question my own experiences at its more difficult passages. <br /><br />On a general note, however, please stop titling book's <em>The Blank's Daughter</em>. From the abortionist to the gravedigger to the bonesetter, I'm tired of women being defined by the occupation of their father. What shall I title my memoir? <em>The Senior Health-care Analyst's Daughter</em>? Hmm. . . . Regardless, after <em>Joy Luck Club</em>, this is definitely Tan's most powerful novel. Bravo. <br /><br />Some passages that struck a chord:<br /><br />There's a lovely discussion on someone's favorite word, vapors, a passage too long for me to quote, but very thoughtfully done (pgs. 20-21).<br /><br />"A lot of her [mother's] admonitions had to do with <em>not</em> showing what you really felt about all sorts of things: hope disappointment , and especially love. The less you showed, the more you meant" (p. 92). Or in my own mother's case, the less you showed, the more you were in control of your feelings, your effect on others, and the situation involved: a misguided philosophy I took years to unlearn, though I know it's hopeless to convince my mom of the error of her affective formula. <br /><br />"'You can have pride in what you do each day, [. . .] ut not arrogance in what you were born with" (p. 250).<br /><br />And lastly, "It broke her heart to see her mother trying so hard, being so conscientious, do determine to be valuable. Making her mother happy would have been easy all along. LuLing simply wanted to be essential, as a mother should be" (p. 301). </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9222294057579649007">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20810097" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1109476-irish" class="left imgcol" title="Irish"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208980941p2/1109476.jpg" alt="Irish" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/20810097?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1109476-irish" class="user" title="Irish">Irish</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">very patient people who are tolerant of meh characters.</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer20810097">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3843932670054176066">This was the first <a title="Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Amy%20Tan">Amy Tan</a> book I read. This book wasn't specifically recommended, but the author was. I was expecting something magical to happen as I turned the pages, but I couldn't get past the first four or five chapters of the book. Besides the overly long sections of actionless description (the story stagnated because of a poor balance between backstory, scene setup and description, and actual let's-move-things-along plot), the main character Ruth is so weak and whiny that I couldn't empa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3843932670054176066">This was the first <a title="Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Amy%20Tan">Amy Tan</a> book I read. This book wasn't specifically recommended, but the author was. I was expecting something magical to happen as I turned the pages, but I couldn't get past the first four or five chapters of the book. Besides the overly long sections of actionless description (the story stagnated because of a poor balance between backstory, scene setup and description, and actual let's-move-things-along plot), the main character Ruth is so weak and whiny that I couldn't empathize, sympathize or even remotely identify with her; she made it impossible to get into the novel. It may be unfair to give The Bonesetter's Daughter a poor review without reading the whole thing, but I wonder how anyone could stay with this character for any length of time. I did like the character of LuLing, even if the stilted, stereotypical dialog coming from her seemed unecessary at best and amateurish at worst. LuLing, Ruth's aging and Alzheimer-stricken mother, is a strong character and the only thing that kept me in the novel as long as I was. <br /><br />Bottom line: the protagonist was forgettable and the pace was too slow. Even January molasses memoirs get somewhere, but this book just ended up back at the library well ahead of its due date.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3843932670054176066">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_20822690">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20822690" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1109907-holly" class="left imgcol" title="Holly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208986867p2/1109907.jpg" alt="Holly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/20822690?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 24, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1109907-holly" class="user" title="Holly">Holly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Holly by: </span>
      My Mother
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1109907-holly?shelf=amy-tan" class="actionLinkLite">amy-tan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer20822690">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6769258173255604237">At the beginning of Amy Tan's fourth novel, two packets of papers written in Chinese calligraphy fall into the hands of Ruth Young. One bundle is titled Things I Know Are True and the other, Things I Must Not Forget. The author? That would be the protagonist's mother, LuLing, who has been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. In these documents the elderly matriarch, born in China in 1916, has set down a record of her birth and family history, determined to keep the facts from vanishing as her min</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6769258173255604237">At the beginning of Amy Tan's fourth novel, two packets of papers written in Chinese calligraphy fall into the hands of Ruth Young. One bundle is titled Things I Know Are True and the other, Things I Must Not Forget. The author? That would be the protagonist's mother, LuLing, who has been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. In these documents the elderly matriarch, born in China in 1916, has set down a record of her birth and family history, determined to keep the facts from vanishing as her mind deteriorates.<br />A San Francisco career woman who makes her living by ghostwriting self-help books, Ruth has little idea of her mother's past or true identity. What's more, their relationship has tended to be an angry one. Still, Ruth recognizes the onset of LuLing's decline--along with her own remorse over past rancor--and hires a translator to decipher the packets. She also resolves to "ask her mother to tell her about her life. For once, she would ask. She would listen. She would sit down and not be in a hurry or have anything else to do."<br /><br />Framed at either end by Ruth's chapters, the central portion of The Bonesetter's Daughter takes place in China in the remote, mountainous region where anthropologists discovered Peking Man in the 1920s. Here superstition and tradition rule over a succession of tiny villages. And here LuLing grows up under the watchful eye of her hideously scarred nursemaid, Precious Auntie. As she makes clear, it's not an enviable setting: I noticed the ripe stench of a pig pasture, the pockmarked land dug up by dragon-bone dream-seekers, the holes in the walls, the mud by the wells, the dustiness of the unpaved roads. I saw how all the women we passed, young and old, had the same bland face, sleepy eyes that were mirrors of their sleepy minds. Nor is rural isolation the worst of it. LuLing's family, a clan of ink makers, believes itself cursed by its connection to a local doctor, who cooks up his potions and remedies from human bones. And indeed, a great deal of bad luck befalls the narrator and her sister GaoLing before they can finally engineer their escape from China. Along the way, familial squabbles erupt around every corner, particularly among mothers, daughters, and sisters. And as she did in her earlier The Joy Luck Club, Amy Tan uses these conflicts to explore the intricate dynamic that exists between first-generation Americans and their immigrant elders. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6769258173255604237">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14899365">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11150558350095435674">I just didn't enjoy this as much as Amy Tan's other books. Her plot development, with its mother-daughter issues, has become almost a formula. She does do a credible job describing life in China in the last century and I came away with a deeper understanding of that culture. I just never thought of Amy Tan as the Maeve Binchy of Asian writing. This is not meant to be a criticism of Maeve Binchy, an author whose well-written books I think are fun to read. It just is I get the impression that she</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11150558350095435674">I just didn't enjoy this as much as Amy Tan's other books. Her plot development, with its mother-daughter issues, has become almost a formula. She does do a credible job describing life in China in the last century and I came away with a deeper understanding of that culture. I just never thought of Amy Tan as the Maeve Binchy of Asian writing. This is not meant to be a criticism of Maeve Binchy, an author whose well-written books I think are fun to read. It just is I get the impression that she keeps writing the same story, just changing the locations a little and adding nuances to the characters. That is how I am beginning to feel about Amy Tan. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17384480767963335435">Meaningless words are a mere group of letters. And if these words are weaved into a 350+ pages manuscript, the essential plot is misplaced between the evaporation of its characters. <a title="Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5246.Amy_Tan"> Tan</a> exaggeratedly lengthens the stereotypical dilemma of two generations of women (mother- daughter) trying to find solace in a past laden with secrets and customs that mold cultural uprightness. Disappointing outcome to what might have been an admirable chronicle.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/7481492-margitte" class="user" title="Margitte">Margitte</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=community" class="actionLinkLite">community</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=golden-oldies-classics" class="actionLinkLite">golden-oldies-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer544934555">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10978682943903448349">A great read! The mother-daughter relationships spanning over three generations was done so authentically it is hard to believe that Amy Tan was not there herself in each generation living those lives in all the different scenes/eras of the book.<br /><br /><em>"Things I must not forget"</em> - is the first line of some Chinese writings which her mother handed to her and which she managed to translate. <br /><br />Her mother, Luling, was in the early stages of Alzheimers, which forced Ruth (or Lootie as her mother pronounced he</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10978682943903448349">A great read! The mother-daughter relationships spanning over three generations was done so authentically it is hard to believe that Amy Tan was not there herself in each generation living those lives in all the different scenes/eras of the book.<br /><br /><em>"Things I must not forget"</em> - is the first line of some Chinese writings which her mother handed to her and which she managed to translate. <br /><br />Her mother, Luling, was in the early stages of Alzheimers, which forced Ruth (or Lootie as her mother pronounced her name), to finally get someone to translate the rest of the papers. Ruth had a need to understand her mother's behaviour and thoughts better.<br /><br />The book often had too many 'page-filler' detail in. You know, those paragraphs and paragraphs of words, which the reader has to skip here and there to continue the story, but it was written so beautifully, and so detailed, with so much suspense, that I just couldn't put it down.<br /><br />After discovering her mother and grandmother's remarkable life stories hidden in the old Laz-y-Boy chair, Ruth could finally understand herself better, although it was unintended. But she first had to relive two other lives through her mother's meticulous writings to reach a point where she connect all the dots in her own personal relationship-issues with the people around her.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed this book. Excellent read. Amy Tan is not one of those authors who can be put down easily. And to really enjoy her writing style the most, one must make time to sit back and start one of her stories and stay with it right through to the end. It is worth it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10978682943903448349">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_12242829">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12242829" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/770887-holli" class="left imgcol" title="Holli"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1200091454p2/770887.jpg" alt="Holli" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/12242829?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 02, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/770887-holli" class="user" title="Holli">Holli</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/770887-holli?shelf=read-2008" class="actionLinkLite">read-2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer12242829">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4821791807094328042">Like most of Tan's books, this novel focuses on mother-daughter relationships extending over several generations. It is a tale of discovering the truth about our past and ourselves. Ruth's mother LuLing is suffering with the early stages of Alzheimer's and carefully writes down the "Things I Know Are True" and the "Things I Must Not Forget" - leaving them for her daughter to find. These are the vehicles through which Ruth discovers the secrets and truths hidden in her mother's past. This is a wo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4821791807094328042">Like most of Tan's books, this novel focuses on mother-daughter relationships extending over several generations. It is a tale of discovering the truth about our past and ourselves. Ruth's mother LuLing is suffering with the early stages of Alzheimer's and carefully writes down the "Things I Know Are True" and the "Things I Must Not Forget" - leaving them for her daughter to find. These are the vehicles through which Ruth discovers the secrets and truths hidden in her mother's past. This is a wonderful book - a little slow in the beginning but fantastic overall. <br /><br />Definitely read the "Conversation with Amy Tan" in which her friend Molly Giles interviews her (at the end of the novel). This makes the book much more personal and even more wonderful. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_763922416">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/763922416" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5364077-connie" class="left imgcol" title="Connie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" alt="Connie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/763922416?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 20, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5364077-connie" class="user" title="Connie">Connie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=asia" class="actionLinkLite">asia</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=family" class="actionLinkLite">family</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=fall-2013" class="actionLinkLite">fall-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=california" class="actionLinkLite">california</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5364077-connie?shelf=china" class="actionLinkLite">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer763922416">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14520367923590036070">Like <a title="The Joy Luck Club by Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7763.The_Joy_Luck_Club">The Joy Luck Club</a>, this book is about relationships between mothers and daughters, and the importance of knowing each other's life stories. In the first part of the book, we meet Ruth, a first generation Chinese-American working as a ghostwriter for New Age self-help books in California. She has a hard time asserting herself in her ten-year relationship with her boyfriend. Her mother, LuLing, has been recently diagnosed with dementia, and can no longer live alone. LuLing is depressed, criti</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14520367923590036070">Like <a title="The Joy Luck Club by Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7763.The_Joy_Luck_Club">The Joy Luck Club</a>, this book is about relationships between mothers and daughters, and the importance of knowing each other's life stories. In the first part of the book, we meet Ruth, a first generation Chinese-American working as a ghostwriter for New Age self-help books in California. She has a hard time asserting herself in her ten-year relationship with her boyfriend. Her mother, LuLing, has been recently diagnosed with dementia, and can no longer live alone. LuLing is depressed, critical, sends her daughter on guilt trips, and threatens to commit suicide whenever she is crossed. She believes in superstitions and curses, and needs to communicate with the dead when she makes important decisions.<br /><br />The second part of the book tells the story of LuLing and the bonesetter's daughter back in China. This memoir written by LuLing, was my favorite part of the book. LuLing was part of a rural family that made high quality ink that was used in calligraphy. Both LuLing and her mother faced difficult challenges, and were never totally accepted by her father's family. In her teens, LuLing was taken in by missionaries during the Japanese occupation of China, and she later immigrated to the United States. LuLing's journal gives Ruth the knowledge to understand her mother better, and to make sense of Ruth's childhood.<br /><br />The third part of the book is set in the present, and easy solutions are found for both LuLing's and Ruth's problems. A thread seems to tie the three generations of women together in strong, but difficult, mother-daughter relationships.<br /><br />I had mixed feelings about this book. The first part of the book, about Ruth's problems and LuLing's negative parenting, dragged for me. The second part, set in China, was exciting with wonderful characters--the bonesetter grandfather, the wicked relatives, LuLing's first love, the suicidal nursemaid. The short third part brought things together well, but seemed to promise an almost too rosy future.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14520367923590036070">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer116441456">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18358996206564368766">I really enjoyed this one, having wanted to read this one for years. Set in both pre-communist China and modern day California, telling the story of Ruth and her mother LuLing. It is not an easy relationship at all. LuLing is quarrelsome, manipulative, and has made Ruth's life hell for many years. Ruth tries to be understanding, but her mother is driving her crazy and when the doctors say that LuLing is sliding into dementia, Ruth's life turns upside down and leaving her to pick up the pieces. S</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18358996206564368766">I really enjoyed this one, having wanted to read this one for years. Set in both pre-communist China and modern day California, telling the story of Ruth and her mother LuLing. It is not an easy relationship at all. LuLing is quarrelsome, manipulative, and has made Ruth's life hell for many years. Ruth tries to be understanding, but her mother is driving her crazy and when the doctors say that LuLing is sliding into dementia, Ruth's life turns upside down and leaving her to pick up the pieces. Sorting out her mother's rubbish filled home Ruth finds out that what she thought she knew about LuLing may have been quite different. The story is told from two points-of-view, spanning decades from China to America, and full of history, including inkmaking, arranged marriages and Peking Man. Well-written and a hearty recommendation from me. Four stars overall. <br /><br />For the longer review, please go here:<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.epinions.com/review/The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter_by_Amy_Tan_and_by_Copyright_Paperback_Collection_Library_of_Congress_and_narrated_by_Joan_Chen/content_617581547140" target="_blank">http://www.epinions.com/review/The_Bo...</a></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_341348555">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/341348555" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6330462-hildred-billings" class="left imgcol" title="Hildred Billings"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442644055p2/6330462.jpg" alt="Hildred Billings" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/341348555?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6330462-hildred-billings" class="user" title="Hildred Billings">Hildred Billings</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer341348555">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer609345002420327673">"The Bonesetter's Daughter" is the second to last Amy Tan novel I have yet to re-read, and like "Hundred Secret Senses," I realized I couldn't remember a dang thing about this book. "The Joy Luck Club" is all about switching POVs between eight characters, "The Kitchen God's Wife" is basically a super long version of one Joy Luck story (that is of course morbidly depressing half the time), and "Saving Fish From Drowning" is about a ghost following around and narrating about the lulziest tour grou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText609345002420327673">"The Bonesetter's Daughter" is the second to last Amy Tan novel I have yet to re-read, and like "Hundred Secret Senses," I realized I couldn't remember a dang thing about this book. "The Joy Luck Club" is all about switching POVs between eight characters, "The Kitchen God's Wife" is basically a super long version of one Joy Luck story (that is of course morbidly depressing half the time), and "Saving Fish From Drowning" is about a ghost following around and narrating about the lulziest tour group to ever hit Myanmar. Turns out that "Hundred Secret Senses" was about an insufferable woman with a badass sister who had an awesome backstory to tell - turns out that "The Bonesetter's Daughter" is about an insufferable woman with a badass mother who had an awesome backstory to tell.<br /><br />The first thing making Bonesetter Stick out is the fact the daughter's - Ruth - POV is written in third person. Why, when Amy Tan is the queen of rambling first POV? I have no idea. Because the entire middle section narrated by her mother, LuLing, is written in first. But I'm getting ahead of myself.<br /><br />Other than that, it's the usual Amy Tan fare. Ruth is in a miserable relationship on the brink of failure (wow, that's new) and her mother, the immigrant LuLing, drives her bonkers. (That's new too!!) Ruth spends her whole time whining and whining, especially about her mother, and ESPECIALLY about her long-time boyfriend what'shisface. (Oh right, Art. Groan.) Art has two teenaged girls from a previous marriage with the most wtf names ever (Dory and Fia. Yeah. That's real 90s.) who talk like they're six instead of early teens. Basically, Ruth's life is totally moan-worthy and omg all these negative feels. She and Olivia from Hundred Secret Senses should become bffs and complain about how awful it is to be upper middle class in San Francisco.<br /><br />ANYWAY, the story. You see, Ruth has a mother (really?? In an Amy Tan novel??) named LuLing, who is starting to act a little strange. Turns out she has early-onset dementia, and is of course only going to get worse. So what does LuLing do? Write down her entire life story up until moving to America, just in case she forgets any of the details and can never tell her daughter. <br /><br />Ruth has the documents translated while her mother is away. Of course, what she discovers about her mother are things she would have never guessed before. Or even imagined. As usual, LuLing's story about growing up the illegitimate daughter of deformed-by-fire "Bonesetter's Daughter" is both heart wrenching (I mean it's 1920s China come on) and intriguing. I struggled to get through Ruth's set-up chapters and then pretty much devoured all of LuLing's backstory in one night. Since I'd forgotten most of it, it was like it was brand new to me...which is always nice.<br /><br />With all this whining on my part (hi Ruth, you're rubbing off on me) you may be wondering why I gave this book four stars. It's more like 3 and a half, but I decided to round up, because of the score I gave Hundred Secret Senses. These books are almost exactly the same in structure and style, just the details are different. And the biggest difference is that Bonesetter had a waaaay more fulfilling ending than Hundred Secret Senses did. I was actually smiling a little when I closed this book. Unlike the other one which I'm pretty sure I threw across the room. <br /><br />Is it Tan's best work? No. Not at all. I have the "reader's guide" paperback and in it is a lot of mentions on Tan's part about how hard this book was for her to write. Well, yeah. You're pretty much just copying yourself now. (She said affectionately.) That said, Tan is an amazing writer, so her "slush" tends to be far better grade than most other author's magnum opii. I read this book very quickly, not because I'm a speed reader but because I was legit hooked once LuLing's tale began. If you love the backstory's of Tan's "mothers", then read this book now. You won't be disappointed. If you can't bear to read another Olivia-type character again I'm...I'm sorry. Good luck.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="609345002420327673">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/35975.Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Audio CD 9781597770767" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6320013-alysia?shelf=mgr-book-of-the-month" class="actionLinkLite">mgr-book-of-the-month</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer450286737">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12750342183975798460">This is my third Amy Tan book. I have not read a novel from her in years and this book helped me to remember why she is one of my favorite authors. Amy Tan has a timeless writing style. That is the only way I can describe it. She doesn't write overly poetic or too simple. Amy Tan writes with a unique style that is perfect in every way. Her Chinese voice and American Chinese voice interchange with ease.<br />This book addresses mother-daughter relations and the complexes feelings involved. Ruthie is th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12750342183975798460">This is my third Amy Tan book. I have not read a novel from her in years and this book helped me to remember why she is one of my favorite authors. Amy Tan has a timeless writing style. That is the only way I can describe it. She doesn't write overly poetic or too simple. Amy Tan writes with a unique style that is perfect in every way. Her Chinese voice and American Chinese voice interchange with ease.<br />This book addresses mother-daughter relations and the complexes feelings involved. Ruthie is the daughter most of us are. At times, our mom's are not making any sense to us, other times we are just fitting them into our busy lives, while we trying to be mothers to our kids. <br />I love reading books about historical Chinese culture, like Snow Flower and The Secret Fan and The Concubine Saga. This book mixes the past with the present of the women in one family. From generation to generation the reader gets to see why things are the the way they are in one family. Why does Ruthie's mom think she going to be punished? <br />I like the way Amy Tan makes the sequence of events follow so easily in this book. You can see the cause and effect in each chapter.<br />The only reason I am taking a star away, is this book did not have me hooked like her other book Saving Fish From Drowning. Was I missing something? I think it was just the slow start in the beginning. It gets me every time. <br />Overall, a good read. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12750342183975798460">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_116235169">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116235169" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/652392-katje" class="left imgcol" title="Katje"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311811550p2/652392.jpg" alt="Katje" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/116235169?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 10, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/652392-katje" class="user" title="Katje">Katje</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer116235169'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer116235169">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6272535459456240005">Just finished Amy Tan's "The Bonesetter's Daughter." I enjoyed the book for the most part... it felt like her other stories did, a glimpse into a culture radically different from my own, yet wrestling with some similar issues I've grappled with in my own life.<br /><br />The last few pages, though... just drove me insane. This gal is dealing with her elderly Alzheimer-stricken mother with a $750/mo. Social Security income, her insecure boyfriend and his two whiny teenage daughters, losing her confidence in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6272535459456240005">Just finished Amy Tan's "The Bonesetter's Daughter." I enjoyed the book for the most part... it felt like her other stories did, a glimpse into a culture radically different from my own, yet wrestling with some similar issues I've grappled with in my own life.<br /><br />The last few pages, though... just drove me insane. This gal is dealing with her elderly Alzheimer-stricken mother with a $750/mo. Social Security income, her insecure boyfriend and his two whiny teenage daughters, losing her confidence in her career. Then, in the last few pages, magically out of thin air, a huge trust fund appears so the elderly mother can afford the nicest assisted living facility available, a professor instantly decides the elderly mother (the one with dementia) is ideal girlfriend material, the insecure boyfriend becomes Mr. Rock overnight (and his daughters start being wonderful), her career evaporates into thin air as she becomes a novelist, and magically the old mother (who has been wandering the streets in her nightgown, remember) remembers a name she couldn't think of for decades... and the name is backed up by 100-year-old glass photographer plates that somehow survived both WWII and the Chinese cultural revolution.<br /><br />Oh... and eight years into her Alzheimer's, the old mom rings up her daughter and apologizes for what she did to her as a little girl (she can't remember WHAT she did, only that she's supposed to apologize)... the one single thing so many women of my generation long for from their mothers (and in my experience, never ever receive), an acknowledgment of inflicted pain and a request for forgiveness.<br /><br />The absolutely impossible events laid out here just totally destroyed the rest of the entire book for me. The reality for families dealing with dementia is poverty, hurt feelings, loneliness, stress, and (possibly worst of all) unfinished business... the kind that will never, ever be resolved because the people they once knew have dissolved underneath the plaque of the destroyed brains.<br /><br />I felt insulted by the resolution of this book. I can tell it was meant to be heart-warming, satisfying, and hopeful to others in similar positions, but it would be cruel false hope built of wishful thinking and thin air.<br /><br />I think I'm done with Amy Tan.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6272535459456240005">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2208450971934435668"><br />3.5 stars<br />I have always liked Amy Tan's novels. A common theme for her is tension in the mother-daughter relationship. I don't mean this in a volatile way. Her stories are usually stretched around love and understanding, and then forgiveness. In this novel, it seemed that with the strict Asian culture and then with the Americanized child, there seems to be a rift that needs to be bridged. And the author does that beautifully.<br /><br />Her stories aren't fast paced, but, for me, they do evoke feelings of co</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2208450971934435668"><br />3.5 stars<br />I have always liked Amy Tan's novels. A common theme for her is tension in the mother-daughter relationship. I don't mean this in a volatile way. Her stories are usually stretched around love and understanding, and then forgiveness. In this novel, it seemed that with the strict Asian culture and then with the Americanized child, there seems to be a rift that needs to be bridged. And the author does that beautifully.<br /><br />Her stories aren't fast paced, but, for me, they do evoke feelings of compassion. I feel the author knows that many of us haven't escaped childhood without some sort of invisible scars, then with that as a kick-off point, she proceeds to tell a story of tradition, kindness, tough love, forgiveness, compassion, change and above all, understanding.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer423728515751193812">Honestly, this book was not what I expected. Going into it i had my hopes set along the lines of a tale like: <a title="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/930.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha"> <img class="escapedImg" alt="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/books/1388367666s/930.jpg" /></a>.<br /> In some ways, both books are similar and yet in other ways they are separated by gapping differences. I will say I'm glad I went into this without proper enlightenment of it's content, otherwise I would have been reluctant to read it.<br /><br />Now to the highlights (the whole freaking story):<br />This story is recounted in voices of a mother and daughter who are American citizens, but of Chinese dec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText423728515751193812">Honestly, this book was not what I expected. Going into it i had my hopes set along the lines of a tale like: <a title="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/930.Memoirs_of_a_Geisha">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Memoirs of a Geisha by Arthur Golden" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/books/1388367666s/930.jpg" /></a>.<br /> In some ways, both books are similar and yet in other ways they are separated by gapping differences. I will say I'm glad I went into this without proper enlightenment of it's content, otherwise I would have been reluctant to read it.<br /><br />Now to the highlights (the whole freaking story):<br />This story is recounted in voices of a mother and daughter who are American citizens, but of Chinese decent. Ruth, the daughter, is a 46 year old woman who for some part of the book seemed like she was in search of herself. Her mother, LuLing, is superstitious and burdened by the weight of a past-life in her small village in China. These two women are descendants of a famous "Bonesetter". We, as well as Ruth, learn of her heritage and secrets of her ancestry through memories relayed to her by her mother in written pages.<br /><br />MY THOUGHTS:<br /><br />I absolutely, completely, unabashedly adore this book. I really love how the strenuous character of a mother-daughter relationship is portrayed. And with the mix-in of a little bit of filial affection, filial duty and devotion--this book would have been perfect.<br /><br />Themes featured are: Chinese culture, family, ancestry and genealogy, heritage and legacy, ghosts, curses and superstitions, love, self discovery and guilt.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1443421806">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1443421806" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1599338-jennifer" class="left imgcol" title="Jennifer"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442501106p2/1599338.jpg" alt="Jennifer" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1443421806?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1599338-jennifer" class="user" title="Jennifer">Jennifer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1443421806">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13472235486524668422">Amy Tan has an amazing talent for telling stories over time, through generations. She's able to capture the life of a modern woman and the life of that woman's grandmother and make them equally believable. And she is able to do it within the pages of a medium-length novel, not near the length of most historical fiction. Her characters are individuals but also representative of their times. <br /><br />Reading the Bonesetter's Daughter is to be completely immersed, unapologetically, in the world of women. He</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13472235486524668422">Amy Tan has an amazing talent for telling stories over time, through generations. She's able to capture the life of a modern woman and the life of that woman's grandmother and make them equally believable. And she is able to do it within the pages of a medium-length novel, not near the length of most historical fiction. Her characters are individuals but also representative of their times. <br /><br />Reading the Bonesetter's Daughter is to be completely immersed, unapologetically, in the world of women. Her work is feminist in the most broad and basic sense in that these people could never be anything but women, can never escape being women and are that informs everything they do and say. Yet she treats them with care and dignity and puts the highest value on their daily struggles. This is not the only reason to read the book, just the one that struck me the hardest. I found it to be the perfect blend of linguistic arts and plot, storytelling and raw emotion.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13472235486524668422">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1408738377">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14733719911789309250">This is the first Amy Tan book that I have read and I loved it. Her writing is something to be savored and I will be reading more of her books in the future. I laughed out loud and I cried too.<br /><br />Poor Ruth, her struggles with her aging mother, I could relate for sure. As LuLing was from China, my mother is from France; although they are from different countries, having a mother from another country has some of the same similarities and struggles. That faint embarrassment of your mother being differ</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14733719911789309250">This is the first Amy Tan book that I have read and I loved it. Her writing is something to be savored and I will be reading more of her books in the future. I laughed out loud and I cried too.<br /><br />Poor Ruth, her struggles with her aging mother, I could relate for sure. As LuLing was from China, my mother is from France; although they are from different countries, having a mother from another country has some of the same similarities and struggles. That faint embarrassment of your mother being different than the other mothers, wanting to be like the other girls but feeling ashamed for your embarrassment and that fierce love for your mother. Rebelling and trying to be like your friends because you are an American and were born here no matter what your cultural background is. And as you get older, learning how your mother struggled to give you the beautiful life that you have in this country. <br /><br />Ruth finally took the time to learn about her mother and her grandmother's (Precious Auntie) past. The story is heartbreaking and beautiful. I cried as this book ended. I loved the last few sentences and want to remember them, "What is the past but what we choose to remember? ...They can choose not to hide it, to take what is broken, feel the pain and know that it will heal."<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_111499513">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111499513" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2916975-rachel-crooks" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel Crooks"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1257724097p2/2916975.jpg" alt="Rachel Crooks" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/111499513?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 13, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2916975-rachel-crooks" class="user" title="Rachel Crooks">Rachel Crooks</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer111499513">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5085777767233158085">I finally decided to look for The Joy Luck Club at the library – and found The Bonesetter’s Daughter instead. In a way, this book was a similar experience to it – I went into it expecting to find one thing and found another.<br /><br />What I think is expert on Amy Tan’s part is the way she unveils her characters over the course of the book: to begin with, LuLing is the silly, pushy, morose, slightly embarrassing Alzheimer’s-suffering mother of Ruth. Her identity, for being what I thought a stereotypical ve</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5085777767233158085">I finally decided to look for The Joy Luck Club at the library – and found The Bonesetter’s Daughter instead. In a way, this book was a similar experience to it – I went into it expecting to find one thing and found another.<br /><br />What I think is expert on Amy Tan’s part is the way she unveils her characters over the course of the book: to begin with, LuLing is the silly, pushy, morose, slightly embarrassing Alzheimer’s-suffering mother of Ruth. Her identity, for being what I thought a stereotypical version of Chinese motherhood, is easily put in a box. Her appearances in the book come as comic relief to the more complex Ruth’s inner musings. I look no further. I know about all there is to know about Luling and her crazy superstitious idiosyncrasies. Sure, Luling has this memoir that she wrote for her daughter all about her childhood in China, and Ruth (after several years) is finally finding the time to get it translated.<br /><br />And then… <br /><br />Comic relief suddenly becomes poetry. Is this young version of Luling, with her hopes and dreams and failures and disappointments, anything akin to the dumpy elderly woman that I admittedly overlooked? So much of her personality is lost in translation. In America, she is only a semi-fluent mother struggling to make it as a calligrapher, embarrassing her daughter with comments such as “Maybe I die soon!”<br /><br />What is tragic is that I can’t simply pass this off as a story of Chinese immigrants. The story is applicable to mothers and daughters everywhere. Every daughter thinks she is the first to live the story with its dramatic twists and turns. The mother she sees every day of her up-bringing turns into a two-dimensional figure; the complexities and the subtleties are lost. Is age the mask that keeps us from looking further into their lives to find the keys to our own? <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5085777767233158085">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_65796002">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65796002" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1630217-monique" class="left imgcol" title="Monique"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1327353946p2/1630217.jpg" alt="Monique" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65796002?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 07, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1630217-monique" class="user" title="Monique">Monique</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1630217-monique?shelf=books-read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">books-read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65796002">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4333099965277193853">Rating: 3.5 Stars<br /><br />Amy Tan has a gift of writing about the mother and daughter experience. One that transcends race or culture. The Bonesetter's Daughter is about the experience of a daughter coming to terms with her mother's illness and past. Just like the characters in The Joy Luck Club Ruth and her mother LuLing have a difficult relationship. Mostly do to the fact that the mother grow up in China and her daughter was raised in America. It is also a story of a daughter learning to appreciate her</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4333099965277193853">Rating: 3.5 Stars<br /><br />Amy Tan has a gift of writing about the mother and daughter experience. One that transcends race or culture. The Bonesetter's Daughter is about the experience of a daughter coming to terms with her mother's illness and past. Just like the characters in The Joy Luck Club Ruth and her mother LuLing have a difficult relationship. Mostly do to the fact that the mother grow up in China and her daughter was raised in America. It is also a story of a daughter learning to appreciate her mother and culture a little more.<br /><br />The Bonesetter's Daughter is a lot like The Joy Luck Club. It has fewer main characters. But Ruth and LuLing's relationship is almost exactly like that between the mothers and daughters featured in The Joy Luck Club. There is friction because Ruth does not understand her mother. Her mother is from China and after moving to America held on to a lot of her Chinese Culture. LuLing has been in the United States for almost 50 years yet doesn't speak or understand English that well. LuLing is also secretive of her past. All these situations lead to a very strained relationship that leave both Ruth and LuLing feeling unappreciated and misunderstood by one another.<br /><br />The story is told from two points of view. The first person point of view is told by LuLing when she is describing her experience in China. The third person point of view in current times. It is the first person point of view that is the most catching. LuLing voice is powerful. The imagery and language that Tan uses to describe what she (LuLing) went through was fantastic. It expressed not only what the character was going through but the myths and beliefs that are part of Chinese culture. The strength in the story lies in the first person narrative of LuLings story.<br /><br />The one major draw back with the story is that there are a lot of similarities between The Joy Luck Club and The Bonesetter's Daughter. Ruth and LuLing's characters and circumstances could have been switched with any of the mother-daughter pairs in The Joy Luck Club and they would not have been out of place. It makes theme and relationship between them appear recycled and therefore the outcome is not a surprise.<br /><br />Also, there has been criticism online that Tan has a tendency to use stereotypes in her writing. I noticed that in this novel but that could be because I was looking for it.<br /><br />Pros: Imagery, Storyline, Characters<br />Cons: Formula, Stereotypes, Predictable<br /><br />Overall Recommendation:<br /><br />Highly recommended with a precaution: If you have read The Joy Luck Club (or maybe any of her others works) the style and formula are easy noticeable and takes away some of the impact of the story.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4333099965277193853">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/5948569-dee" class="user" title="Dee">Dee</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer193734861">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2610758744310418945">Amy Tan is such an awesome author. She really pulls you in to her stories. I have handed this book to my mother, who thinks she is "loosing it" and wanted her to know what a woman loosing it really experiences. But in this story, the mother seems to tell more truths, that have never been uttered, as he mind starts to go. At first, as Ruth's mother tells the doctor that she is older than Ruth knows her to be, Ruth chalks it all up to her mother's mind going. Then she tells everyone that the woman</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2610758744310418945">Amy Tan is such an awesome author. She really pulls you in to her stories. I have handed this book to my mother, who thinks she is "loosing it" and wanted her to know what a woman loosing it really experiences. But in this story, the mother seems to tell more truths, that have never been uttered, as he mind starts to go. At first, as Ruth's mother tells the doctor that she is older than Ruth knows her to be, Ruth chalks it all up to her mother's mind going. Then she tells everyone that the woman known as her mother (Ruth's grandmother) wasn't really her mother. Ruth becomes more concerned until she finds some writing her mother has done. Written in Chinese, she takes them to someone to translate and begins to learn more about her mother and her life than she had ever imagined. The beauty of her stories is that you don't feel like some drama is unfolding. The stories she tells unfold themselves and as a reader, I feel like I have just been dropped down into her stories to be an observer. I have several of her books and look forward to finally reading the ones I haven't read yet. The Joy Luck Club is an all-time favorite. If you haven't read it, I highly recommend it as well.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_233668084">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/233668084" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5920550-jessica" class="left imgcol" title="Jessica"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443153867p2/5920550.jpg" alt="Jessica" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/233668084?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 08, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5920550-jessica" class="user" title="Jessica">Jessica</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/890415.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780007124442" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer233668084">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11133194118451081798">This is a great book about the mother-daughter relationship and what lurks around its periphery, haunting it. From a critical point of view, I enjoyed the structure which dips between the first narrator Ruth and her mother Lu Ling who becomes the second narrator. Its a story about generations of women who are all disempowered, sometimes in similar ways. <br /><br />Here's a para as a taster of the beauty of Amy Tan's writing: <br />"As he said this, Precious Auntie came back into my mind. I was remembering how sh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11133194118451081798">This is a great book about the mother-daughter relationship and what lurks around its periphery, haunting it. From a critical point of view, I enjoyed the structure which dips between the first narrator Ruth and her mother Lu Ling who becomes the second narrator. Its a story about generations of women who are all disempowered, sometimes in similar ways. <br /><br />Here's a para as a taster of the beauty of Amy Tan's writing: <br />"As he said this, Precious Auntie came back into my mind. I was remembering how she taught me that everything, even ink, had a purpose and a meaning: Good ink cannot be the quick kind, ready to pour out of a bottle. You can never be an artist if your work comes without effort. That is the problem with modern ink from a bottle. You do not have to think. YOu simply write what is swimming on the top of your brain. And the top is nothing but pond scum, dead leaves, and mosquito spawn. But when you push an inkstick along an inkstone,you take the first step to cleansing your mind and your heart. You push and you ask yourself, What are my intentions? What is in my heart that matches my mind?"</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11133194118451081798">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span id="like_container_review_233668084" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/233668084?resource_type=Review" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_review_233668084"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F77649.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" rel="nofollow">Like</a></span> · <a href="/review/show/233668084?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>
