<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_677694946">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/677694946" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7681130-allen-grace" class="left imgcol" title="Allen Grace"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1361861389p2/7681130.jpg" alt="Allen Grace" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/677694946?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7681130-allen-grace" class="user" title="Allen Grace">Allen Grace</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/227676.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780804114981" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7681130-allen-grace?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7681130-allen-grace?shelf=2013-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7681130-allen-grace?shelf=asian-lit" class="actionLinkLite">asian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer677694946">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7692569283578412393"><strong>You should think <br />about your character. <br />Know where you are changing, <br />how you will be changed, <br />what cannot be changed back again...</strong><br /><br /><br /><br />Ahh, words to live by.<br /><br /><br />Allow me to tease you in saying how awesome this book is, how these words can offer you comfort and at the same time pull you in to an estranged place you have never been to, a place that has a different culture where people speak in fragments, a new language you're not familiar with.<br /><br /><br /><strong>The Bonesetter's Daughter</strong> is my very first <em>Amy Tan</em> read and defi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7692569283578412393"><strong>You should think <br />about your character. <br />Know where you are changing, <br />how you will be changed, <br />what cannot be changed back again...</strong><br /><br /><br /><br />Ahh, words to live by.<br /><br /><br />Allow me to tease you in saying how awesome this book is, how these words can offer you comfort and at the same time pull you in to an estranged place you have never been to, a place that has a different culture where people speak in fragments, a new language you're not familiar with.<br /><br /><br /><strong>The Bonesetter's Daughter</strong> is my very first <em>Amy Tan</em> read and definitely won't be the last! I've mentioned this before but it bears repeating, it's love at first page and I knew from that moment I fell in love with how Amy Tan writes.<br /><br />xxxxxxxx<br /><br /><blockquote> Ruth recalled the first day she had walked by herself along this stretch of beach. It had been clean, untrampled. She had felt the waves, cold and shocking, grab at her ankles, wanting to pull her in. She remembered how she had cried with relief as the waves roared around her.<br /><br />As she now kept walking, she felt comforted by the water, its constancy, its predictability. Each time it withdrew, it carried with it whatever had marked the shore. </blockquote><br /><br /><br />I was sitting on the floor, and my back against the wall near the window and my book was propped up on my knees when I came across with this beautiful description of Ruth treading her soft footsteps on the sand, utterly confused and helpless. That night I had imagined myself to be that woman in the sea of pages, walking along the stretch of the beach, drowning myself in words, needing to hear the pummeling waves like it's only the source of breath I'd only take.<br /><br />xxxxxxxx<br /><br />The novel started off with a promise but after a few chapters it seemed that I lost my way in the middle. I tried few attempts to abandon it, leave it on my shelf and decide to just read it when the mood comes back, however had it not been for LuLing Young's retelling story and my book buddy's slight persuasion I could have left it for good. <br /><br />My exposure to Chinese culture is very limited (to Chinese food only). Seriously. Don’t you just love Chinese food? <br /><br /><br />But what I liked about this book was that it’s pretty much engaging and evocative. It's well-written, the words carved by beauty and exquisite details that left me in awe. It's LuLing Young's story that kept me going even up to the last page and parts of her story saddened me. I got the feeling that the writer knows what to say and use it to her advantage. I loved LuLing Young, her strengths and weaknesses, her failures and achievements, her gestures and facial expressions when she gets mad, her talking in fragments because she can't form a well-constructive sentence in English. I also liked Ruth and Art, their exchanged conversations, about vapors and especially on how they met. I felt giddy remembering how Ruth perceived him as gay because of some misplaced conclusion she couldn't help herself but be embarrased about it. I guess it's true what they say about first impressions, they don't last.<br /><br /><br />Overall, it's an interesting read. A must read to those who are exorbitantly besotted by the Asian literature. I highly recommend it! I can't seem to remember a non-essential character in the story because even the side characters as well are worth remembering and very relatable. Precious Auntie and Kai Jing hold a special place in my heart, always. However, there are scenes that were a bit off-puting and irrelevant in the story that made me cringe and wonder at the same time. The ending did not disappoint, though so I don't really care that much.<br /><br /><br />This novel will urge you to reflect on yourself and how well do you know your mother. This isn't about a family's curse, or superstitions. It's about love and forgiveness. Let go of your guilt feelings and start achieving your dreams. Learn to appreciate youself in the best way you can and just let go of your past so you can create your future. This novel will inspire you that happiness need not to come from the outside world, it comes from within. Also, it will teach you to find effortless beauty even from the smallest, intricate things that at first you have no knowledge of but the point is to be always searching and looking and be humble for knowledge. <br /><br />I am an ardent fan of words and I always believe in the power of words. Amy Tan has this way with words that I instantly feel connected. I am excited and thrilled to read her other novels.<br /><br /><br />In the meantime, here are some of my favourite quotes from the novel that I liked and if I could list them all, I probably would have written the entire novel itself. :)<br /><br /><br /><em> "A mother is always the beginning. She is how things begin."</em><br /><br /><em> "There are no such things as curses, those are superstitions, and superstitions is a needless fear. The only curses are worries you can't get rid of." </em><br /><br /><em> "You can have pride in what you do each day, but not arrogance in what you were born with." </em><br /><br /><em> "A person should consider how things begin. A particular beginning results in a particular end."</em><br /><br /><em> "Ruth wondered what made people happy. Couldn't you find happiness in a place? In another person? What about happiness for herself? Did you simply have to know what you wanted and reach for it through the fog?" </em><br /><br /><br />This is how I imagined Luling Young would look like. . .<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1133.photobucket.com/user/LaGrazie/media/chinese_zpsc4f7c75a.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo chinese_zpsc4f7c75a.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380430622r/863437._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><em> I was not there when this happened, yet I saw it. The only way I could push it out of my mind was to go into my memory. And there in that safe place, I was with him, and he was kissing me when he told me, <strong>"We are divine, unchanged by time"</strong>.</em><br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>So, what's your favorite word?</strong><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7692569283578412393">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/743091-karen" class="user" title="Karen">Karen</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer265632343">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1853199232065844900">I read "The Joy Luck Club" when it was popular 5 or 6 years ago. It was good, but didn't cause me to deliberately seek out Tan's other books. A year or so ago, my oldest daughter left a copy of "The Bonesetter's Daughter" on our coffee table. The few times she's been at home since then, she always asked if I'd read it yet. Well, I finally did. To my amazement, I was completely sucked in to the portion of the story involving the grandmother and mother when they lived in "old" China, pre-WWII, mor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1853199232065844900">I read "The Joy Luck Club" when it was popular 5 or 6 years ago. It was good, but didn't cause me to deliberately seek out Tan's other books. A year or so ago, my oldest daughter left a copy of "The Bonesetter's Daughter" on our coffee table. The few times she's been at home since then, she always asked if I'd read it yet. Well, I finally did. To my amazement, I was completely sucked in to the portion of the story involving the grandmother and mother when they lived in "old" China, pre-WWII, more so than I was by the tale of the Chinese-American daughter, who would be the character with whom I would be most closely expected to identify. This is a novel about mothers and daughters. While my relationships with my own mother and my daughters weren't/aren't nearly as tumultuous as those in this novel, I did recognize enough similarities in the characters feelings and actions that it was almost eerie. If you are a woman who had a mother and have an adult daughter, you should enjoy this book. I'm still pondering exactly what my daughter was trying to tell me...</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_325852632">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/325852632" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9314075-carl-r" class="left imgcol" title="Carl R."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1336319811p2/9314075.jpg" alt="Carl R." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/325852632?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 07, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9314075-carl-r" class="user" title="Carl R.">Carl R.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer325852632">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11913229917709261559"> I sort of skipped The Bonesetter’s Daughter when it came out a few years ago despite the fact that I’ve met Tan and her husband and hiked a Sierra trail with them and their two Yorkies (I wasn't alone with them, of course. There were a number of others along, it being a writers’ workshop event.) You might think these would be inducements to run out and get everything that fell from her pen. However, she had this kids’ TV series about cats, and The Joy Luck Club (movie and book) didn’t enthrall</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11913229917709261559"> I sort of skipped The Bonesetter’s Daughter when it came out a few years ago despite the fact that I’ve met Tan and her husband and hiked a Sierra trail with them and their two Yorkies (I wasn't alone with them, of course. There were a number of others along, it being a writers’ workshop event.) You might think these would be inducements to run out and get everything that fell from her pen. However, she had this kids’ TV series about cats, and The Joy Luck Club (movie and book) didn’t enthrall me, and on the trail she made infantile jokes with her dogs (They know how to talk. What goes on top of a house. woof. See?) So I left Bonesetter alone. Then came the word that the San Francisco Opera has commissioned Stewart Wallace (Harvey Milk) to compose an opera based on the book, with libretto by Ms. Tan herself. I’m certainly not going to miss that, and I suddenly didn’t want to miss the book either.<br /> After the first hundred pages or so, I wondered where in the world an opera was going to come from. It’s mostly a routine household drama about a couple of mid-forties live-togethers named Ruth and Art. He has two teen-age daughters whose custody alternates between their divorced parents. She has a difficult aging mother who is sliding into dementia. <br /> To my mind Tan takes far too long in the telling of this rather ordinary setup. However, don’t stop reading because the rewards for continuing are rich indeed. In the process of combing through her mother’s belongings, Ruth uncovers a manuscript in Chinese, in her mother’s hand. She has it translated, and in this book-within-the-book is a reading treasure that has the makings of quite a grand opera.<br /> The mother’s autobiographical tale is a story that begins in a small village in pre-WWII northern China, goes from there to Peking, to Shanghai, to Hong Kong, and on to San Francisco. The story is filled with elements of lust, avarice, betrayal, self-mutilation, revenge, suicide, love, devotion, perseverance and just about every other element of human vice and virtue imaginable. Even though you know most of how it turns out, it’s filled with suspense, and some of the scenes will tear at your heart.<br /> The return to the framing drama in San Francisco, is inevitably anti-climatic, but the web of mother-daughter relationships, the complex mixes of love and rejection and fear of love and rejection take on such dimension and meaning once we’ve experienced that inside story that even the limited dynamics of the Ruth-Art fable transcend their TV level mundanity.<br /> Not a perfect book, but a very worthwhile one, and definitely worthy of an opera. I can’t wait.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11913229917709261559">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/15785717-hannah" class="left imgcol" title="Hannah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1356527798p2/15785717.jpg" alt="Hannah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/489811643?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 30, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/15785717-hannah" class="user" title="Hannah">Hannah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer489811643">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7699933193521711756">I found the first section of the book which introduces the reader to the protagonist, Ruth, quite difficult to pursue, she wasn't immediately likeable to me and I didn't find these chapters fleshed out very much. Ruth's idiosyncrasies, such as her listing her to-do tasks on her hands, I just didn't find functional to the character's development or seem that believable in fact. Maybe I am missing some symbolism in this?<br /><br />The middle section of the book I really liked and the historical context very</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7699933193521711756">I found the first section of the book which introduces the reader to the protagonist, Ruth, quite difficult to pursue, she wasn't immediately likeable to me and I didn't find these chapters fleshed out very much. Ruth's idiosyncrasies, such as her listing her to-do tasks on her hands, I just didn't find functional to the character's development or seem that believable in fact. Maybe I am missing some symbolism in this?<br /><br />The middle section of the book I really liked and the historical context very much interested me. I found the mother-daughter relationship this part a lot more interesting and I guess it is in this section that the drama really unfolds.<br /><br />I thought <em>The Bonesetter's Daughter</em> finished in a well-wrapped kind of way, but I actually felt as though the book could have done with being longer, having felt that many of the non mother-daughter relationships could have highlighted and given more impact to parts of the story had they been padded out more.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7699933193521711756">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/1607230-shelleyrae-at-book-d-out" class="user" title="Shelleyrae at Book'd Out">Shelleyrae at Book'd Out</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer34941046">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10638709683100497499">Amy Tan has a way of getting inside mother daughter relationships that is startling. All of her novels explore the bond at both its best and worst. Part of what makes her stories so interesting is the clash of culture and of generational change which is so different to my own. The Bonesetters Daughter is probably the darkest of her novels, despite the (too) neat ending. The stories of the women are fascinating, though I had a hard time liking Ruth much which is probably unfair, I know all too we</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10638709683100497499">Amy Tan has a way of getting inside mother daughter relationships that is startling. All of her novels explore the bond at both its best and worst. Part of what makes her stories so interesting is the clash of culture and of generational change which is so different to my own. The Bonesetters Daughter is probably the darkest of her novels, despite the (too) neat ending. The stories of the women are fascinating, though I had a hard time liking Ruth much which is probably unfair, I know all too well how difficult mother daughter relationships can be.<br />It is the type of book that makes you want to ring your mother or grandmother and ask questions about their life that you hadn't thought to ever ask.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10638709683100497499">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer723227988389842395">I loved that I started reading this--with its interesting metaphor of fire and water coming together to make steam--while on vacation to West Yellowstone, in particular on the day we visited the geysers.<br /><br />Amy Tan's mother-daughter conflicts speak to me in ways that don't need explaining. This episode did not disappoint. My only regret (is it experience or simply jealousy?) is that everything wrapped up so happily at the end in such a perfectly tied bow. I'm not so good at tying bows and not so hop</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText723227988389842395">I loved that I started reading this--with its interesting metaphor of fire and water coming together to make steam--while on vacation to West Yellowstone, in particular on the day we visited the geysers.<br /><br />Amy Tan's mother-daughter conflicts speak to me in ways that don't need explaining. This episode did not disappoint. My only regret (is it experience or simply jealousy?) is that everything wrapped up so happily at the end in such a perfectly tied bow. I'm not so good at tying bows and not so hopeful for such a perfect ending in real life. But I guess I shouldn't begrudge anyone else that kind of happy ending.</span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer912834858">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3448514970723640093">The first Amy Tan book I've read, and while it isn't exactly light reading, she has a beautiful way of writing. It's one book that'll keep you turning pages despite the late hour and early work schedule the next day.<br />Written across three generations of women, it brings out the cultural differences (in China at least) yet shows us that it doesn't take much to understand what a person felt. Because effectually, all feelings are human.<br />But the way the story meanders, it really makes you question how</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3448514970723640093">The first Amy Tan book I've read, and while it isn't exactly light reading, she has a beautiful way of writing. It's one book that'll keep you turning pages despite the late hour and early work schedule the next day.<br />Written across three generations of women, it brings out the cultural differences (in China at least) yet shows us that it doesn't take much to understand what a person felt. Because effectually, all feelings are human.<br />But the way the story meanders, it really makes you question how society treats people. And that evey person has a reason for the way they are<br />Never forget your roots.</span>

  <a href="/user/show/1899699-nora" class="left imgcol" title="Nora"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1234312118p2/1899699.jpg" alt="Nora" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/48749944?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 09, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1899699-nora" class="user" title="Nora">Nora</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12555.The_Bonesetter_s_Daughter" title="Paperback 9780345457370" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer48749944">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5592408639808262930">This book is about 3 generations of Chinese women and their experiences in China and in the United States. Its main characters are an elderly immigrant who is losing her memory, her American daughter, and her traditional Chinese mother. I love Amy Tan! Her descriptions of Chinese people, their experiences, and the way they talk and look at the world are always fascinating to me. They also match both people I know and histories or biographies I've read. This is especially compelling. It even has</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5592408639808262930">This book is about 3 generations of Chinese women and their experiences in China and in the United States. Its main characters are an elderly immigrant who is losing her memory, her American daughter, and her traditional Chinese mother. I love Amy Tan! Her descriptions of Chinese people, their experiences, and the way they talk and look at the world are always fascinating to me. They also match both people I know and histories or biographies I've read. This is especially compelling. It even has a positive ending.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6oqHorfXpTQha2qbdzRZN8wZWb0uo+rNo/A3UOnyVPaPXrhxRQWxzq2zHiAJVb+Fhe6apGd+rWfJ+TLCJWeisw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('13D/qP17P4a7DNeDG1WGDs/urpEYRthsmVBnSeuK8u6ypMB7D6krfDfUozhlNGC8hhltiFGbn8bzWWLbJx8Eqw==')})

Inside the Forbidden City, she witnessed seductions, plots, murders, and brazen acts of treason. Propelled by a shrewd intelligence, an extraordinary persistence, and a friendship with the imperial heir, she rose through the ranks to become the first Empress of China. On the one hand, she was a political mastermind who quelled insurrections, eased famine, and opened wide the routes of international trade. On the other, she was a passionate patron of the arts who brought Chinese civilization to unsurpassed heights of knowledge, beauty, and sophistication.\n\nAnd yet, from the moment of her death to the present day, her name has been sullied, her story distorted, and her memoirs obliterated by men taking vengeance on a women who dared become Emperor. For the first time in thirteen centuries, Empress Wu flings open the gates of her Forbidden City and tells her own astonishing tale–revealing a fascinating, complex figure who in many ways remains These are stories which Xinran&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText4969901901455575058\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Message from an Unknown Chinese Mother is made up of the stories of Chinese mothers whose daughters have been wrenched from them, and also brings us the voices of some adoptive mothers from different parts of the world. These are stories which Xinran could not bring herself to tell previously - because they were too painful and close to home. In the footsteps of Xinran\'s Good Women of China, this is personal, immediate, full of harrowing, tragic detail but also uplifting, tender moments.\n\nTen chapters, ten women and many stories of heartbreak, including her own: Xinran once again takes us right into the lives of Chinese women - students, successful business women, midwives, peasants, all with memories which have stained their lives. Whether as a consequence of the single-child policy, destructive age-old traditions or hideous economic necessity... these women had to give up their daughters for adoption, others were forced to abandon them - on city streets, outside hospitals, orphanages or on station platforms - and others even had to watch their baby daughters being taken away at birth, and drowned. Here are the \'extra-birth guerrillas\' who travel the roads and the railways, evading the system, trying to hold onto more than one baby; naive young student girls who have made life-wrecking mistakes; the \'pebble mother\' on the banks of the Yangzte still looking into the depths for her stolen daughter; peasant women rejected by their families because they can\'t produce a male heir; and finally there is Little Snow, the orphaned baby fostered by Xinran but \'confiscated\' by the state.\n\nThe book sends a heartrending message from their birth mothers to all those Chinese girls who have been adopted overseas (at the end of 2006 there were over 120,000 registered adoptive families for Chinese orphans, almost all girls, in 27 countries), to show them how things really were for Buck\'s epic tells the history of Korea through the lives of one family.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5246.Amy_Tan">About Amy Tan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/5246.Amy_Tan"><img alt="5246" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1437607346p2/5246.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer9418596075348615639">Amy Tan (Chinese: ???; pinyin: Tán ?nm?i; born February 19, 1952) is an American writer whose works explore mother-daughter relationships and what it means to grow up as a first generation Asian American. In 1993, Tan's adaptation of her most popular fiction work, The Joy Luck Club, became a commercially successful film.<br /><br />She has written several other books, including The Kitchen God's Wife, The Hun</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9418596075348615639">Amy Tan (Chinese: ???; pinyin: Tán ?nm?i; born February 19, 1952) is an American writer whose works explore mother-daughter relationships and what it means to grow up as a first generation Asian American. In 1993, Tan's adaptation of her most popular fiction work, The Joy Luck Club, became a commercially successful film.<br /><br />She has written several other books, including The Kitchen God's Wife, The Hundred Secret Senses, and The Bonesetter's Daughter, and a collection of non-fiction essays entitled The Opposite of Fate: A Book of Musings. Her most recent book, Saving Fish From Drowning, explores the tribulations experienced by a group of people who disappear while on an art expedition into the jungles of Burma. In addition, Tan has written two children's books: The Moon Lady (1992) and Sagwa, The Chinese Siamese Cat (1994), which was turned into an animated series airing on PBS. She has also appeared on PBS in a short spot on encouraging children to write.<br /><br />Currently, she is the literary editor for West, Los Angeles Times' Sunday magazine.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9418596075348615639">...more</a>

    </div>
